</div>
<p>PepsiCo's product mix as of 2015 (based on worldwide net revenue) consists of 53 percent foods, and 47 percent beverages.<sup id="cite_ref-Report15_P10_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report15_P10-50">[50]</a></sup> On a worldwide basis, the company's current products lines include several hundred brands that in 2009 were estimated to have generated approximately $108 billion in cumulative annual <a href="/wiki/Retail_sales" class="mw-redirect" title="Retail sales">retail sales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-annualrpt09_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annualrpt09-49">[49]</a></sup></p><p>The primary identifier of a food and beverage industry main brand is annual sales over $1 billion. As of 2015, 22 PepsiCo brands met that mark, including: <a href="/wiki/Pepsi" title="Pepsi">Pepsi</a>, Diet Pepsi, <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Dew" title="Mountain Dew">Mountain Dew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lay%27s" title="Lay's">Lay's</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gatorade" title="Gatorade">Gatorade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tropicana_Products" title="Tropicana Products">Tropicana</a>, <a href="/wiki/7_Up" title="7 Up">7 Up</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doritos" title="Doritos">Doritos</a>, Brisk, <a href="/wiki/Quaker_Oats_Company" title="Quaker Oats Company">Quaker Foods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheetos" title="Cheetos">Cheetos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mirinda" title="Mirinda">Mirinda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruffles" title="Ruffles">Ruffles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquafina" title="Aquafina">Aquafina</a>, Naked, Kevita, Propel, Sobe, H2oh, Sabra, <a href="/wiki/Starbucks" title="Starbucks">Starbucks</a> (ready to Drink Beverages), <a href="/wiki/Pepsi_Max" title="Pepsi Max">Pepsi Max</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tostitos" title="Tostitos">Tostitos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Mist" title="Sierra Mist">Sierra Mist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fritos" title="Fritos">Fritos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Walkers_(snack_foods)" title="Walkers (snack foods)">Walkers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(4)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Business_divisions">Business divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Business divisions" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-4 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-4">
<p>The structure of PepsiCo's global operations has shifted multiple times in its history as a result of international expansion, and as of 2016 it is separated into six main divisions: <i>PepsiCo Beverages North America</i>, <i>Frito-Lay North America</i>, <i>Quaker Foods North America</i>, <i>Latin America</i>, <i>Europe and Sub-Saharan African</i>, and <i>Asia, Middle East and North Africa</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> As of 2015, 73 percent of the company's <a href="/wiki/Net_revenue" class="mw-redirect" title="Net revenue">net revenues</a> came from North and South America; 17 percent from Europe and Sub-Saharan Africa; and 10 percent from Asia, the Middle East, and Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Report15_P10_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report15_P10-50">[50]</a></sup> PepsiCo and its combined subsidiaries employed approximately 263,000 people worldwide as of December 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="PepsiCo_Beverages_North_America">PepsiCo Beverages North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: PepsiCo Beverages North America" data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>This division contributed 35 percent of PepsiCo's net revenue as of 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-Report15_P10_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report15_P10-50">[50]</a></sup> and involves the manufacture (and in some cases <a href="/wiki/Licensing" class="mw-redirect" title="Licensing">licensing</a>), marketing and sales of both carbonated and non-carbonated beverages in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-pabgorham_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pabgorham-54">[54]</a></sup> The main brands distributed under this division include <a href="/wiki/Pepsi" title="Pepsi">Pepsi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Dew" title="Mountain Dew">Mountain Dew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gatorade" title="Gatorade">Gatorade</a>, <a href="/wiki/7_Up" title="7 Up">7 Up</a> (outside the U.S.), <a href="/wiki/Tropicana_Products" title="Tropicana Products">Tropicana</a> Pure Premium orange juice, <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Mist" title="Sierra Mist">Sierra Mist</a>, <a href="/wiki/SoBe" title="SoBe">SoBe</a> Lifewater, Tropicana juice drinks, <a href="/wiki/AMP_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="AMP Energy">AMP Energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naked_Juice" title="Naked Juice">Naked Juice</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Izze" title="Izze">Izze</a>. <a href="/wiki/Aquafina" title="Aquafina">Aquafina</a>, the company's bottled water brand, is also marketed and licensed through North America Beverages.<sup id="cite_ref-companyprofilereuters_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-companyprofilereuters-55">[55]</a></sup> In 2015, PepsiCo also introduced <a href="/wiki/Stubborn_Soda" class="mw-redirect" title="Stubborn Soda">Stubborn Soda</a>, a line of carbonated beverages without <a href="/wiki/High_fructose_corn_syrup" class="mw-redirect" title="High fructose corn syrup">high fructose corn syrup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p><p>PepsiCo also has formed partnerships with several beverage brands it does not own, in order to distribute or market them with its own brands.<sup id="cite_ref-companyprofilereuters_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-companyprofilereuters-55">[55]</a></sup> As of 2010, its partnerships include: <a href="/wiki/Starbucks" title="Starbucks">Starbucks</a> (Frappuccino, DoubleShot, and Iced Coffee), <a href="/wiki/Unilever" title="Unilever">Unilever</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Lipton" title="Lipton">Lipton</a> brand (Lipton Brisk and Lipton Iced Tea), and <a href="/wiki/Dole_Food_Company" title="Dole Food Company">Dole</a> (licensed juices and drinks).
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Frito-Lay_North_America">Frito-Lay North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Frito-Lay North America" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p><i>Frito-Lay North America</i>, the result of a merger in 1961 between the Frito Company and the H.W. Lay Company, produces the top selling line of snack foods in the U.S. Its main brands in the U.S., Canada, and Mexico and include <a href="/wiki/Lay%27s" title="Lay's">Lay's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruffles" title="Ruffles">Ruffles</a> potato chips; <a href="/wiki/Doritos" title="Doritos">Doritos</a> tortilla chips; <a href="/wiki/Tostitos" title="Tostitos">Tostitos</a> tortilla chips and dips; <a href="/wiki/Cheetos" title="Cheetos">Cheetos</a> cheese flavored snacks; <a href="/wiki/Fritos" title="Fritos">Fritos</a> corn chips; <a href="/wiki/Rold_Gold" title="Rold Gold">Rold Gold</a> pretzels; <a href="/wiki/Sun_Chips" title="Sun Chips">Sun Chips</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Cracker_Jack" title="Cracker Jack">Cracker Jack</a> popcorn. Products made by this division are sold to independent distributors and retailers, and are transported from Frito-Lay's manufacturing plants to distribution centers, principally in vehicles owned and operated by the company.<sup id="cite_ref-flnayahoo_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flnayahoo-57">[57]</a></sup></p><p>The division contributed 23 percent of PepsiCo's net revenue in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Report15_P10_50-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report15_P10-50">[50]</a></sup> Until November 2009, Christopher Furman, President of Ventura Foods Inc., occupied the position of Food Services CEO.<sup id="cite_ref-annualrpt09_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annualrpt09-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pepsize_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pepsize-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pepkeyfacts_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pepkeyfacts-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Quaker_Foods_North_America">Quaker Foods North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Quaker Foods North America" data-section="11" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p><i>Quaker Foods North America</i>, created following PepsiCo's acquisition of the <a href="/wiki/Quaker_Oats_Company" title="Quaker Oats Company">Quaker Oats Company</a> in 2001, manufactures, markets, and sells Quaker Oatmeal, <a href="/wiki/Rice-A-Roni" title="Rice-A-Roni">Rice-A-Roni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cap%27n_Crunch" title="Cap'n Crunch">Cap'n Crunch</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Life_(cereal)" title="Life (cereal)">Life cereals</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> side dishes within North America. This division also owns and produces the <a href="/wiki/Aunt_Jemima" title="Aunt Jemima">Aunt Jemima</a> brand, which as of 2009 was the top selling line of <a href="/wiki/Syrup" title="Syrup">syrups</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pancake" title="Pancake">pancake</a> mixes within this region.<sup id="cite_ref-10k_41-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10k-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-qfnahist_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-qfnahist-60">[60]</a></sup></p><p><i>Sabritas</i> and <i>Gamesa</i> are two of PepsiCo's food and snack business lines headquartered in Mexico, and they were acquired by PepsiCo in 1966 and 1990, respectively. <a href="/wiki/Sabritas" title="Sabritas">Sabritas</a> markets Frito-Lay products in Mexico, including local brands such as Poffets, Rancheritos, Crujitos, and Sabritones. <a href="/wiki/Gamesa" title="Gamesa">Gamesa</a> is the largest manufacturer of <a href="/wiki/Cookies" class="mw-redirect" title="Cookies">cookies</a> in Mexico, distributing brands such as Emperador, Arcoiris and Marías Gamesa.<sup id="cite_ref-sabritasgamesa_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sabritasgamesa-61">[61]</a></sup></p><p>The division contributed 4 percent of PepsiCo's net revenues in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Report15_P10_50-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report15_P10-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Latin America" data-section="12" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>PepsiCo's <i>Latin America Foods</i> (Spanish: <i>Snacks América Latina</i>) operations market and sell primarily Quaker- and Frito-Lay-branded snack foods within <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central</a> and South America, including Argentina, Brazil, Peru, and other countries in this region.<sup id="cite_ref-peplatin_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peplatin-62">[62]</a></sup> Snacks América Latina purchased Peruvian company Karinto S.A.C. including its production company Bocaditas Nacionales (with three production facilities in Peru) from the Hayashida family of Lima in 2009, adding the Karito brand to its product line, including Cuates, Fripapas, and Papi Frits.<sup id="cite_ref-karinto_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-karinto-63">[63]</a></sup></p><p>The company started a new market strategy to sell its Pepsi Cola product in Mexico, stating that about one third of the population has difficulty pronouncing "Pepsi". With manufacture and sales of its product under the label 'Pécsi', the advertisement campaign features the Mexican soccer celebrity <a href="/wiki/Cuauht%C3%A9moc_Blanco" title="Cuauhtémoc Blanco">Cuauhtémoc Blanco</a>. In 2009, PepsiCo had previously used the same strategy successfully in Argentina.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p><p>Pepsico will market and distribute <a href="/wiki/Starbucks" title="Starbucks">Starbucks</a> products in several Latin American countries for 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p><p>The division contributed 13 percent of PepsiCo's net revenues in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-Report15_P10_50-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report15_P10-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe_and_Sub-Saharan_Africa">Europe and Sub-Saharan Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Europe and Sub-Saharan Africa" data-section="13" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>PepsiCo began to expand its distribution in Europe in the 1980s, and in 2015 it made up 17 percent of the company's global net revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-Report15_P10_50-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report15_P10-50">[50]</a></sup> Unlike PepsiCo's Americas business segments, both foods and beverages are manufactured and marketed under one umbrella division in this region, known as <i>PepsiCo Europe</i>. The primary brands sold by PepsiCo in Europe include Pepsi-Cola beverages, Frito-Lay snacks, Tropicana juices, and Quaker food products, as well as regional brands unique to Europe such as <a href="/wiki/Walkers_crisps" class="mw-redirect" title="Walkers crisps">Walkers crisps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copella" title="Copella">Copella</a>, Paw Ridge, Snack-a-Jack, <a href="/wiki/Duyvis" title="Duyvis">Duyvis</a>, and others. PepsiCo also produces and distributes the soft drink 7UP in Europe via license agreement. Pepsico has 3 sites in South Africa (Isando, Parrow, and Prospecton) which produce Lay's and Simba chips.<sup id="cite_ref-companyprofilereuters_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-companyprofilereuters-55">[55]</a></sup></p><p>PepsiCo's European presence expanded in Russia in 2009 as the company announced a $1B investment,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and with its acquisition of Russian juice and dairy product brand <a href="/wiki/Wimm-Bill-Dann_Foods" title="Wimm-Bill-Dann Foods">Wimm-Bill-Dann Foods</a> in December 2010 and <a href="/wiki/Lebedyansky_(company)" title="Lebedyansky (company)">Lebedyansky</a> juice producer in March 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-wimmbilldan_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wimmbilldan-68">[68]</a></sup></p><p>According to the Reuters, "PepsiCo reported that in 2017, its <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian</a> operations generated net revenue of $3.23 billion, which made up 5.1 percent of the company’s total net revenue."<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia_and_North_Africa">Asia and North Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Asia and North Africa" data-section="14" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>The most recently created operating division of PepsiCo covers Asia, the Middle East and Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-q32010earnings_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-q32010earnings-42">[42]</a></sup> In addition to the production and sales of several worldwide Pepsi-Cola, Quaker Foods, and Frito-Lay beverage and food product lines (including Pepsi and Doritos), this segment of PepsiCo's business markets regional brands such as <a href="/wiki/Mirinda" title="Mirinda">Mirinda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurkure" title="Kurkure">Kurkure</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Smith%27s_Snackfood_Company" title="The Smith's Snackfood Company">Red Rock Deli</a>, among others.<sup id="cite_ref-companyprofilereuters_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-companyprofilereuters-55">[55]</a></sup> While PepsiCo owns its own manufacturing and distribution facilities in certain parts of these regions, more of this production is conducted via alternate means such as <a href="/wiki/Licensing" class="mw-redirect" title="Licensing">licensing</a> (which it does with Aquafina), contract manufacturing, joint ventures, and <a href="/wiki/Affiliate_(commerce)" title="Affiliate (commerce)">affiliate</a> operations. PepsiCo's businesses in these regions, as of 2015, contributed 10 percent to the company's <a href="/wiki/Net_revenue" class="mw-redirect" title="Net revenue">net revenue</a> worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-Report15_P10_50-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Report15_P10-50">[50]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2012, PepsiCo signed an agreement with a local Myanmar distributor to sell its soft drinks after a 15-year break to re-enter the country.<sup id="cite_ref-burmanew_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burmanew-70">[70]</a></sup></p><p>SodaStream, which PepsiCo acquired in 2018 is based in Israel, while <a href="/wiki/Sabra_(company)" title="Sabra (company)">Sabra</a> (which PepsiCo co-owns with the Israeli food conglomerate <a href="/wiki/Strauss_Group" title="Strauss Group">Strauss Group</a>) holds a 60% market share for <a href="/wiki/Hummus" title="Hummus">hummus</a> sales in the United States as of 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The Strauss Group produces and distributes Frito-Lay products in Israel.
</p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_governance">Corporate governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Corporate governance" data-section="15" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">
<p>Headquartered in  <a href="/wiki/Harrison,_New_York" title="Harrison, New York">Harrison, New York</a>, in the hamlet of <a href="/wiki/Purchase,_New_York" title="Purchase, New York">Purchase</a>, with research and development headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Valhalla,_New_York" title="Valhalla, New York">Valhalla</a>, New York, PepsiCo's Chairman and CEO is <a href="/wiki/Ramon_Laguarta" title="Ramon Laguarta">Ramon Laguarta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The board of directors is composed of eleven outside directors as of 2010, including <a href="/wiki/Ray_Lee_Hunt" title="Ray Lee Hunt">Ray Lee Hunt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shona_Brown" title="Shona Brown">Shona Brown</a>, <a href="/wiki/Victor_Dzau" title="Victor Dzau">Victor Dzau</a>, Arthur C. Martinez, <a href="/wiki/Sharon_Percy_Rockefeller" title="Sharon Percy Rockefeller">Sharon Percy Rockefeller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Vasella" title="Daniel Vasella">Daniel Vasella</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dina_Dublon" title="Dina Dublon">Dina Dublon</a>, Ian M. Cook, <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Ibarg%C3%BCen" title="Alberto Ibargüen">Alberto Ibargüen</a>, and Lloyd G. Trotter. Former top executives at PepsiCo include <a href="/wiki/Steven_Reinemund" title="Steven Reinemund">Steven Reinemund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Enrico" title="Roger Enrico">Roger Enrico</a>, <a href="/wiki/D._Wayne_Calloway" title="D. Wayne Calloway">D. Wayne Calloway</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Sculley" title="John Sculley">John Sculley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_H._Jordan" title="Michael H. Jordan">Michael H. Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Donald_M._Kendall" title="Donald M. Kendall">Donald M. Kendall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_A._Sinclair" title="Christopher A. Sinclair">Christopher A. Sinclair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irene_Rosenfeld" title="Irene Rosenfeld">Irene Rosenfeld</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_C._Novak" title="David C. Novak">David C. Novak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brenda_C._Barnes" title="Brenda C. Barnes">Brenda C. Barnes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Steele" title="Alfred Steele">Alfred Steele</a>.
</p><p>On October 1, 2006, former Chief Financial Officer and President <a href="/wiki/Indra_Nooyi" title="Indra Nooyi">Indra Nooyi</a> replaced <a href="/wiki/Steve_Reinemund" class="mw-redirect" title="Steve Reinemund">Steve Reinemund</a> as Chief Executive Officer. Nooyi remained as the corporation's president, and became <a href="/wiki/Chair_(official)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chair (official)">Chairman of the Board</a> in May 2007, later (in 2010) being named No.1 on <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'s</span> list of the "50 Most Powerful Women"<sup id="cite_ref-fortune50_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fortune50-74">[74]</a></sup> and No.6 on <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span> list of the "World's 100 Most Powerful Women".<sup id="cite_ref-forbeswom_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forbeswom-75">[75]</a></sup> PepsiCo received a 100 percent rating on the <a href="/wiki/Corporate_Equality_Index" title="Corporate Equality Index">Corporate Equality Index</a> released by the <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a>-advocate group <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Campaign" title="Human Rights Campaign">Human Rights Campaign</a> starting in 2004, the third year of the report.<sup id="cite_ref-eqindex_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eqindex-76">[76]</a></sup></p><p>In November 2014, the firm's president Zein Abdalla announced he would be stepping down from his position at the firm by the end of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p><p>In 2017, Ramon Laguarta became the president and became its CEO in 2018.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Headquarters">Headquarters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Headquarters" data-section="16" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>The PepsiCo headquarters are located in the hamlet of <a href="/wiki/Purchase,_New_York" title="Purchase, New York">Purchase, New York</a>, in the town and village of <a href="/wiki/Harrison,_New_York" title="Harrison, New York">Harrison, New York</a>. It was one of the last architectural works by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Durell_Stone" title="Edward Durell Stone">Edward Durell Stone</a>. It consists of seven three-story buildings. Each building is connected to its neighbor through a corner. The property includes the <a href="/wiki/Donald_M._Kendall_Sculpture_Gardens" title="Donald M. Kendall Sculpture Gardens">Donald M. Kendall Sculpture Gardens</a> with 45 contemporary sculptures open to the public. Works include those of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Calder" title="Alexander Calder">Alexander Calder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moore" title="Henry Moore">Henry Moore</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Rodin" title="Auguste Rodin">Auguste Rodin</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Westchester_Magazine" title="Westchester Magazine">Westchester Magazine</a></i> stated "The buildings' square blocks rise from the ground into low, inverted ziggurats, with each of the three floors having strips of dark windows; patterned pre-cast concrete panels add texture to the exterior surfaces." In 2010 the magazine ranked the building as one of the ten most beautiful buildings in Westchester County.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p><p>At one time, PepsiCo had its headquarters in 500 <a href="/wiki/Park_Avenue_(Manhattan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Park Avenue (Manhattan)">Park Avenue</a> in <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">Midtown Manhattan</a>, New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> In 1956 PepsiCo paid $2 million for the original building.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> PepsiCo built the new 500 Park Avenue in 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-Bunshaft_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bunshaft-81">[81]</a></sup> In 1966, <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_New_York_City" title="Mayor of New York City">Mayor of New York City</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Lindsay" title="John Lindsay">John Lindsay</a> started a private campaign to convince PepsiCo to remain in New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Six months later, the company announced that it was moving to 112 acres (45 ha) of the Blind Brook Polo Club in Westchester County.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> After PepsiCo left the Manhattan building, it became known as the Olivetti Building.<sup id="cite_ref-Bunshaft_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bunshaft-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Charitable_activities">Charitable activities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Charitable activities" data-section="17" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plaza_Venezuela,_Caracas.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Plaza_Venezuela%2C_Caracas.jpg/220px-Plaza_Venezuela%2C_Caracas.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1739" data-file-height="1153"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 146px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Plaza_Venezuela%2C_Caracas.jpg/220px-Plaza_Venezuela%2C_Caracas.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="146" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plaza_Venezuela,_Caracas.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Headquarters of Pepsi-Cola Venezuela (<a href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pepsi-Cola_Venezuela" class="extiw" title="es:Pepsi-Cola Venezuela">ES</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>PepsiCo has maintained a philanthropic program since 1962 called the PepsiCo Foundation,<sup id="cite_ref-found2_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-found2-84">[84]</a></sup> in which it primarily funds "nutrition and activity, safe water and water usage efficiencies,<sup id="cite_ref-winston_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-winston-85">[85]</a></sup> and education," according to the foundation's website.<sup id="cite_ref-found_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-found-86">[86]</a></sup> In 2009, $27.9 million was contributed through this foundation, including grants to the <a href="/wiki/United_Way_of_America" title="United Way of America">United Way</a><sup id="cite_ref-newmf_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newmf-87">[87]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/YMCA" title="YMCA">YMCA</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ymca_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ymca-88">[88]</a></sup> among others.
</p><p>In 2009, PepsiCo launched an initiative called the <i>Pepsi Refresh Project</i>, For the first time in 23 years, PepsiCo did not invest in Superbowl advertising for its iconic brand. Instead, the company diverted this $20 million to the social media-fueled Pepsi Refresh Project: PepsiCo's innovative cause-marketing program in which consumers submitted ideas for grants for health, environmental, social, educational, and cultural causes<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-grin_ton_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grin_ton-90">[90]</a></sup> in which individuals submit and vote on charitable and <a href="/wiki/Nonprofit" class="mw-redirect" title="Nonprofit">nonprofit</a> collaborations.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The main recipients of grants as part of the refresh project are community organizations with a local focus and nonprofit organizations, such as a high school in <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> that—as a result of being selected in 2010—received $250,000 towards construction of a fitness room.<sup id="cite_ref-michigan_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-michigan-92">[92]</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_Horizon_oil_spill" title="Deepwater Horizon oil spill">Gulf of Mexico oil spill</a> in the spring of 2010, PepsiCo donated $1.3 million to grant winners determined by popular vote.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt2_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt2-93">[93]</a></sup> As of October 2010, the company had provided a cumulative total of $11.7 million in funding, spread across 287 ideas of participant projects from 203 cities in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-gainesville_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gainesville-94">[94]</a></sup> In late 2010, the refresh project was reported to be expanding to include countries outside of North America in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-refreshintl_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-refreshintl-95">[95]</a></sup></p><p><br></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(6)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_record">Environmental record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Environmental record" data-section="18" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-6 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-6">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Rainforests_and_palm_oil">Rainforests and palm oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Rainforests and palm oil" data-section="19" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>PepsiCo Palm Oil Commitments<sup id="cite_ref-PepsiCo_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PepsiCo-96">[96]</a></sup> published in May 2014 were welcomed by media as a positive step towards ensuring that the company's <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a> purchases will not contribute to deforestation and human rights abuses in the palm oil industry. NGOs warned<sup id="cite_ref-RAN_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RAN-97">[97]</a></sup> that the commitments did not go far enough, and in light of the deforestation crisis in Southeast Asia, have called on the company to close the gaps in its policies immediately.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetically_modified_ingredients">Genetically modified ingredients</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Genetically modified ingredients" data-section="20" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>PepsiCo has contributed $1,716,300 to oppose the passage of California Proposition 37, which would mandate the disclosure of <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">genetically modified crops</a> used in the production of California food products.<sup id="cite_ref-Huffington_Post_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huffington_Post-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MapLight_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MapLight-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-San_Diego_Reader_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-San_Diego_Reader-100">[100]</a></sup> PepsiCo believes "that genetically-modified products can play a role in generating positive economic, social and environmental contributions to societies around the world; particularly in times of food shortages."<sup id="cite_ref-proxy_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-proxy-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Water_usage_.28India.2C_U.S..2C_U.K..29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_usage_(India,_U.S.,_U.K.)">Water usage (India, U.S., U.K.)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Water usage (India, U.S., U.K.)" data-section="21" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>PepsiCo's usage of water was the subject of controversy in India in the early and mid-2000s, in part because of the company's alleged impact on water usage in a country where <a href="/wiki/Water_shortage" class="mw-redirect" title="Water shortage">water shortages</a> are a perennial issue. In this setting, PepsiCo was perceived by India-based environmental organizations as a company that diverted water to manufacture a discretionary product, making it a target for critics at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-busweekasia_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-busweekasia-102">[102]</a></sup></p><p>As a result, in 2003 PepsiCo launched a country-wide program to achieve a "positive water balance" in India by 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-chadha_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chadha-103">[103]</a></sup> In 2007, PepsiCo's CEO Indra Nooyi made a trip to India to address water usage practices in the country, prompting prior critic <a href="/wiki/Sunita_Narain" title="Sunita Narain">Sunita Narain</a>, director of the Centre for Science &amp; Environment (CSE), to note that PepsiCo "seem(s) to be doing something serious about water now."<sup id="cite_ref-busweekasia_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-busweekasia-102">[102]</a></sup> According to the company's 2009 corporate citizenship report,<sup id="cite_ref-susrept_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-susrept-104">[104]</a></sup> as well as media reports at the time,<sup id="cite_ref-warner_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warner-105">[105]</a></sup> the company (in 2009) replenished nearly six billion liters of water within India, exceeding the aggregate water intake of approximately five billion liters by PepsiCo's India manufacturing facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-susrept_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-susrept-104">[104]</a></sup></p><p>Water usage concerns have arisen at times in other countries where PepsiCo operates. In the U.S., <a href="/wiki/Water_shortage" class="mw-redirect" title="Water shortage">water shortages</a> in certain regions resulted in increased scrutiny on the company's production facilities, which were cited in media reports as being among the largest water users in cities facing drought—such as <a href="/wiki/Atlanta,_Georgia" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlanta, Georgia">Atlanta, Georgia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-glennon_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-glennon-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wheatley_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wheatley-107">[107]</a></sup> In response, the company formed partnerships with non-profit organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/Earth_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth Institute">Earth Institute</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water.org" title="Water.org">Water.org</a>, and in 2009 began cleaning new Gatorade bottles with purified air instead of rinsing with water, among other <a href="/wiki/Water_conservation" title="Water conservation">water conservation</a> practices.<sup id="cite_ref-gardner_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gardner-108">[108]</a></sup> In the United Kingdom, also in response to regional <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a> conditions, PepsiCo snacks brand Walkers' reduced water usage at its largest <a href="/wiki/Potato_chip" title="Potato chip">potato chip</a> facility by 45 percent between the years 2001 and 2008. In doing so, the factory used machinery that captured water naturally contained in potatoes, and used it to offset the need for outside water.<sup id="cite_ref-potatowater_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-potatowater-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-guardianwater_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardianwater-110">[110]</a></sup></p><p>As a result of water reduction practices and efficiency improvements, PepsiCo in 2009 saved more than 12 billion liters of water worldwide,<sup id="cite_ref-water2_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-water2-111">[111]</a></sup> compared to its 2006 water usage. Environmental advocacy organizations including the <a href="/wiki/Natural_Resources_Defense_Council" title="Natural Resources Defense Council">Natural Resources Defense Council</a> and individual critics such as <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Anderson" title="Rocky Anderson">Rocky Anderson</a> (mayor of <a href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City,_Utah" class="mw-redirect" title="Salt Lake City, Utah">Salt Lake City, Utah</a>) voiced concerns in 2009, noting that the company could conserve additional water by refraining from the production of discretionary products such as Aquafina.<sup id="cite_ref-gunther_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gunther-112">[112]</a></sup> The company maintained its positioning of bottled water as "healthy and convenient", while also beginning to partially offset environmental impacts of such products through alternate means, including <a href="/wiki/Packaging" class="mw-redirect" title="Packaging">packaging</a> weight reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-gunther_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gunther-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Pesticide_regulation_.28India.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Pesticide_regulation_(India)">Pesticide regulation (India)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Pesticide regulation (India)" data-section="22" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pesticide_regulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Pesticide regulation">Pesticide regulation</a></div>
<p>PepsiCo's India operations were met with substantial resistance in 2003 and again in 2006, when an environmental organization in New Delhi made the claim that, based on its research, it believed that the levels of <a href="/wiki/Pesticides" class="mw-redirect" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a> in PepsiCo (along with those from rival The Coca-Cola Company), exceeded a set of proposed safety standards on soft drink ingredients that had been developed by the Bureau of Indian Standards.<sup id="cite_ref-bremner1_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bremner1-113">[113]</a></sup> PepsiCo denied the allegations, and India's health ministry has also dismissed the allegations—both questioning the accuracy of the data compiled by the CSE, as it was tested by its own internal laboratories without being verified by outside peer review.<sup id="cite_ref-reeves_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reeves-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bremner2_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bremner2-115">[115]</a></sup> The ensuing dispute prompted a short-lived ban on the sale of PepsiCo and The Coca-Cola Company soft drinks within India's southwestern state of <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a> in 2006;<sup id="cite_ref-bbc1_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc1-116">[116]</a></sup> however this ban was reversed by the Kerala High Court one month later.<sup id="cite_ref-bhatt_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bhatt-117">[117]</a></sup></p><p>In November 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_India" title="Supreme Court of India">Supreme Court of India</a> invalidated a criminal complaint filed against PepsiCo India by the <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a> government, on the basis that the beverages did meet local standards at the time of the allegations. The court ruling stated that the "percentage of pesticides" found in the tested beverages was "within the tolerance limits subsequently prescribed in respect of such product," since at the time of testing "there was no provision governing pesticide adulteration in cold drinks."<sup id="cite_ref-bustandard_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bustandard-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-indiatimes_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indiatimes-119">[119]</a></sup> In 2010, PepsiCo was among the 12 multinational companies that displayed "the most impressive corporate social responsibility credentials in emerging markets", as determined by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_State" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of State">U.S. Department of State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-roemer_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-roemer-120">[120]</a></sup> PepsiCo's India unit received recognition on the basis of its water conservation and safety practices and corresponding results.<sup id="cite_ref-warcx_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warcx-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Packaging_and_recycling">Packaging and recycling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Packaging and recycling" data-section="23" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Environmental advocates have raised concern over the environmental impacts surrounding the disposal of PepsiCo's bottled beverage products in particular, as bottle <a href="/wiki/Recycling" title="Recycling">recycling</a> rates for the company's products in 2009 averaged 34 percent within the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-grnbiz_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grnbiz-122">[122]</a></sup> In 2019, <a href="/w/index.php?title=BreakFreeFromPlastic&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="BreakFreeFromPlastic (page does not exist)">BreakFreeFromPlastic</a> named PepsiCo a top 10 global plastic polluter for the second year in a row.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> The company has employed efforts to minimize these environmental impacts via <a href="/wiki/Packaging" class="mw-redirect" title="Packaging">packaging</a> developments combined with recycling initiatives. In 2010, PepsiCo announced a goal to create partnerships that prompt an increase in the beverage container recycling rate in the U.S. to 50 percent by 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-nusca_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nusca-124">[124]</a></sup></p><p>One strategy enacted to reach this goal has been the placement of interactive recycling kiosks called "Dream Machines" in supermarkets, <a href="/wiki/Convenience_stores" class="mw-redirect" title="Convenience stores">convenience stores</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gas_stations" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas stations">gas stations</a>, with the intent of increasing access to recycling receptacles.<sup id="cite_ref-kiosk1_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kiosk1-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kiosk2_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kiosk2-126">[126]</a></sup> The use of <a href="/wiki/Resin" title="Resin">resin</a> to manufacture its <a href="/wiki/Plastic_bottle" title="Plastic bottle">plastic bottles</a> has resulted in reduced packaging weight, which in turn reduces the volume of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a> required to transport certain PepsiCo products. The weight of Aquafina bottles was reduced nearly 40 percent, to 15 grams, with a packaging redesign in 2009. Also in that year, PepsiCo brand <a href="/wiki/Naked_Juice" title="Naked Juice">Naked Juice</a> began production and distribution of the first 100 percent <a href="/wiki/PET_bottle_recycling" title="PET bottle recycling">post-consumer recycled plastic bottle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-schwartz_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schwartz-127">[127]</a></sup></p><p>On March 15, 2011, PepsiCo unveiled the world's first plant-based <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_terephthalate" title="Polyethylene terephthalate">PET</a> bottle. The bottle is made from plant-based materials, such as <a href="/wiki/Switch_grass" class="mw-redirect" title="Switch grass">switch grass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corn_husks" class="mw-redirect" title="Corn husks">corn husks</a>, and pine bark, and is 100% recyclable. PepsiCo plans to reuse more by-products of its manufacturing processes such as orange peels and oat hulls in the bottles. PepsiCo has identified methods to create a molecular structure that is the same as normal petroleum-based PET—which will make the new bottle technology, dubbed "Green Bottle", feel the same as normal PET. PepsiCo will pilot production in 2012, and upon successful completion of the pilot, intends moving to full-scale commercialization.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_usage_and_carbon_footprint">Energy usage and carbon footprint</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Energy usage and carbon footprint" data-section="24" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>PepsiCo, along with other manufacturers in its industry, has drawn criticism from environmental advocacy groups for the production and distribution of plastic product packaging, which consumed an additional 1.5 billion US gallons (5,700,000 m<sup>3</sup>) of <a href="/wiki/Petrochemicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Petrochemicals">petrochemicals</a> in 2008. These critics have also expressed apprehension over the production volume of plastic packaging, which results in the <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">emission</a> of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-geller_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geller-129">[129]</a></sup> Beginning largely in 2006, PepsiCo began development of more efficient means of producing and distributing its products using less energy,<sup id="cite_ref-bruce_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bruce-130">[130]</a></sup> while also placing a focus on <a href="/wiki/Emissions_reduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Emissions reduction">emissions reduction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-geller_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-geller-129">[129]</a></sup> In a comparison of 2009 <a href="/wiki/Energy_usage" class="mw-redirect" title="Energy usage">energy usage</a> with recorded usage in 2006, the company's per-unit use of energy was reduced by 16 percent in its beverage plants and 7 percent in snack plants.<sup id="cite_ref-susrept_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-susrept-104">[104]</a></sup></p><p>In 2009, Tropicana (owned by PepsiCo) was the first brand in the U.S. to determine the carbon footprint of its <a href="/wiki/Orange_juice" title="Orange juice">orange juice</a> product, as certified by the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_Trust" title="Carbon Trust">Carbon Trust</a>, an outside auditor of carbon emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-martin_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-martin-131">[131]</a></sup> Also in 2009, PepsiCo began the test deployment of so-called "green <a href="/wiki/Vending_machines" class="mw-redirect" title="Vending machines">vending machines</a>", which reduce energy usage by 15 percent in comparison to average models in use. It developed these machines in coordination with <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a>, which described the initiative as "transforming the industry in a way that is going to be more <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>-friendly to a great degree."<sup id="cite_ref-martin_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-martin-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(7)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Product_nutrition">Product nutrition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Product nutrition" data-section="25" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-7 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-7">
<p>According to its 2009 annual report, PepsiCo states that it is "committed to delivering sustainable growth by investing in a healthier future for people and our planet,"<sup id="cite_ref-annualrpt09_49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annualrpt09-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-susrept_104-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-susrept-104">[104]</a></sup> which it has defined in its mission statement since 2006 as "Performance with Purpose".<sup id="cite_ref-anreport06_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anreport06-132">[132]</a></sup> According to news and magazine coverage on the subject in 2010, the objective of this initiative is to increase the number and variety of healthier food and beverage products made available to its customers,<sup id="cite_ref-econperf_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econperf-133">[133]</a></sup> employ a reduction in the company's <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental impact">environmental impact</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-clancy_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clancy-134">[134]</a></sup> and to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">diversity</a> and healthy lifestyles within its employee base.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Its activities in regards to the pursuit of its goals—namely environmental impacts of production and the nutritional composition of its products—have been the subject of recognition from health and environmental advocates and organizations, and at times have raised concerns among its critics. As the result of a more recent focus on such efforts, "critics consider (PepsiCo) to be perhaps the most proactive and progressive of the food companies", according to former <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> food industry writer Melanie Warner in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-warner_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-warner-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Product_diversity">Product diversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Product diversity" data-section="26" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>From its founding in 1965 until the early 1990s, the majority of PepsiCo's product line consisted of carbonated soft drinks and convenience <a href="/wiki/Snacks" class="mw-redirect" title="Snacks">snacks</a>. PepsiCo broadened its product line substantially throughout the 1990s and 2000s with the <a href="/wiki/Takeover" title="Takeover">acquisition</a> and development of what its CEO deemed as "good-for-you" products, including <a href="/wiki/Quaker_Oats" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaker Oats">Quaker Oats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naked_Juice" title="Naked Juice">Naked Juice</a>, and Tropicana orange juice.<sup id="cite_ref-mangalindan_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mangalindan-135">[135]</a></sup> Sales of such healthier-oriented PepsiCo brands totaled $10 billion in 2009, representing 18 percent of the company's total revenue in that year. This movement into a broader, healthier product range has been moderately well received by nutrition advocates; though commentators in this field have also suggested that PepsiCo market its healthier items as aggressively as less-healthy core products.<sup id="cite_ref-moore_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moore-136">[136]</a></sup></p><p>In response to shifting consumer preferences and in part due to increasing governmental regulation, PepsiCo in 2010 indicated its intention to grow this segment of its business, forecasting that sales of fruit, vegetable, <a href="/wiki/Whole_grain" title="Whole grain">whole grain</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fiber" title="Fiber">fiber</a>-based products will amount to $30 billion by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-confinogoals_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-confinogoals-137">[137]</a></sup> To meet this intended target, the company has said that it plans to acquire additional health-oriented brands while also making changes to the composition of existing products that it sells.<sup id="cite_ref-confinogoals_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-confinogoals-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Ingredient_changes_in_Pepsi">Ingredient changes in Pepsi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ingredient changes in Pepsi" data-section="27" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Public health advocates have suggested that there may be a link between the <a href="/wiki/Ingredient" title="Ingredient">ingredient</a> makeup of PepsiCo's core snack and carbonated soft drink products and rising rates of health conditions such as <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>. The company aligns with personal <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/responsibility" class="extiw" title="wikt:responsibility">responsibility</a> advocates, who assert that food and beverages with higher proportions of sugar or salt content are fit for consumption in moderation by individuals who also exercise on a regular basis.<sup id="cite_ref-byrnes_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byrnes-138">[138]</a></sup></p><p>Changes to the composition of its products with nutrition in mind have involved reducing fat content, moving away from <a href="/wiki/Trans-fats" class="mw-redirect" title="Trans-fats">trans-fats</a>, and producing products in <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calorie</a>-specific <a href="/wiki/Serving_size" title="Serving size">serving sizes</a> to discourage overconsumption, among other changes.<sup id="cite_ref-econperf_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-econperf-133">[133]</a></sup> One of the earlier ingredient changes involved sugar and caloric reduction, with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Diet_Pepsi" title="Diet Pepsi">Diet Pepsi</a> in 1964 and <a href="/wiki/Pepsi_Max" title="Pepsi Max">Pepsi Max</a> in 1993—both of which are variants of their full-calorie counterpart, <a href="/wiki/Pepsi" title="Pepsi">Pepsi</a>. More recent changes have consisted of <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fat</a> reduction, which <a href="/wiki/Frito-Lay" title="Frito-Lay">Frito-Lay</a> reduced by 50% in <a href="/wiki/Lay%27s" title="Lay's">Lay's</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruffles" title="Ruffles">Ruffles</a> potato chips in the U.S. between 2006 and 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-byrnes_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byrnes-138">[138]</a></sup> Also in 2009, PepsiCo's Tropicana brand introduced a new variation of orange juice (Trop50) sweetened in part by the plant <a href="/wiki/Stevia" title="Stevia">Stevia</a>, which reduced calories by half.<sup id="cite_ref-byrnes_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-byrnes-138">[138]</a></sup> Since 2007, the company also made available lower-calorie variants of <a href="/wiki/Gatorade" title="Gatorade">Gatorade</a>, which it calls "G2".<sup id="cite_ref-gatoradeg2_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gatoradeg2-139">[139]</a></sup> On May 5, 2014, PepsiCo announced that the company would remove the flame retardant chemical known as "Brominated Vegetable Oil" from many of its products, but a time-frame was not discussed.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_to_children">Distribution to children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Distribution to children" data-section="28" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>As public perception placed additional scrutiny on the marketing and distribution of carbonated soft drinks to children, PepsiCo announced in 2010 that by 2012, it will remove beverages with higher sugar content from primary and secondary schools worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-kcity_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kcity-141">[141]</a></sup> It also, under voluntary guidelines adopted in 2006, replaced "full-calorie" beverages in U.S. schools with "lower-calorie" alternatives, leading to a 95 percent reduction in the 2009 sales of full-calorie variants in these schools in comparison to the sales recorded in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-pepsugarschools_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pepsugarschools-142">[142]</a></sup> In 2008, in accordance with guidelines adopted by the International Council of Beverages Associations, PepsiCo eliminated the advertising and marketing of products that do not meet its nutrition standards, to children under the age of 12.<sup id="cite_ref-fredrix_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fredrix-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gilbert2_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gilbert2-144">[144]</a></sup></p><p>In 2010, <a href="/wiki/First_Lady" title="First Lady">First Lady</a> <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Obama" title="Michelle Obama">Michelle Obama</a> initiated a campaign to end <a href="/wiki/Childhood_obesity" title="Childhood obesity">childhood obesity</a> (titled <i><a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Move!" title="Let's Move!">Let's Move!</a></i>), in which she sought to encourage healthier food options in <a href="/wiki/Public_school_(government_funded)" class="mw-redirect" title="Public school (government funded)">public schools</a>, improved food <a href="/wiki/Nutrition_labeling" class="mw-redirect" title="Nutrition labeling">nutrition labeling</a>, and increased <a href="/wiki/Physical_activity" title="Physical activity">physical activity</a> for children. In response to this initiative, PepsiCo, along with food manufacturers <a href="/wiki/Campbell_Soup" class="mw-redirect" title="Campbell Soup">Campbell Soup</a>, Coca-Cola, <a href="/wiki/General_Mills" title="General Mills">General Mills</a>, and others in an alliance referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Healthy_Weight_Commitment_Foundation" title="Healthy Weight Commitment Foundation">Healthy Weight Commitment Foundation</a>", announced in 2010 that the companies will collectively cut one trillion calories from their products sold by the end of 2012 and 1.5 trillion calories by the end of 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-black_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-black-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(8)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=PepsiCo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: See also" data-section="29" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-8 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-8">
